    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_da7e7dcf-0d38-41a6-aba1-1744c8bef0e7'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13945829666697613110"><i>Man in the Dark </i>is Paul Auster’s brilliant, devastating novel about the many realities we inhabit as wars flame all around us.<br/><br/>Seventy-two-year-old August Brill is recovering from a car accident in his daughter’s house in Vermont. When sleep refuses to come, he lies in bed and tells himself stories, struggling to push back thoughts about things he would prefer to forget—his</span>
  <span id="freeText13945829666697613110" style="display:none"><i>Man in the Dark </i>is Paul Auster’s brilliant, devastating novel about the many realities we inhabit as wars flame all around us.<br /><br />Seventy-two-year-old August Brill is recovering from a car accident in his daughter’s house in Vermont. When sleep refuses to come, he lies in bed and tells himself stories, struggling to push back thoughts about things he would prefer to forget—his wife’s recent death and the horrific murder of his granddaughter’s boyfriend, Titus. The retired book critic imagines a parallel world in which America is not at war with Iraq but with itself. In this other America the twin towers did not fall and the 2000 election results led to secession, as state after state pulled away from the union and a bloody civil war ensued. As the night progresses, Brill’s story grows increasingly intense, and what he is so desperately trying to avoid insists on being told. Joined in the early hours by his granddaughter, he gradually opens up to her and recounts the story of his marriage. After she falls asleep, he at last finds the courage to revisit the trauma of Titus’s death.<br /><br />Passionate and shocking, <i>Man in the Dark </i>is a novel of our moment, a book that forces us to confront the blackness of night even as it celebrates the existence of ordinary joys in a world capable of the most grotesque violence.<br /><br />*<i>Time Out </i>(Chicago)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13945829666697613110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_153357595" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/153357595" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/153357595?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer153357595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7482546863040562452">“I am alone in the dark.” One of the more evocative existential opening lines. And it tells much about what follows. August Brill is 72, a Pulitzer Prize winning book critic who is confined to a wheelchair, and mourning the loss of his wife. While convalescing, he is living with his divorced daughter and his granddaughter, Katya, who is mourning the horrible death of her boyfriend in Iraq. We see the events of the story through Brill’s eyes. In attempting to overcome a bout of insomnia, and perh</span>
  <span id="freeText7482546863040562452" style="display:none">“I am alone in the dark.” One of the more evocative existential opening lines. And it tells much about what follows. August Brill is 72, a Pulitzer Prize winning book critic who is confined to a wheelchair, and mourning the loss of his wife. While convalescing, he is living with his divorced daughter and his granddaughter, Katya, who is mourning the horrible death of her boyfriend in Iraq. We see the events of the story through Brill’s eyes. In attempting to overcome a bout of insomnia, and perhaps escape the dark place in which he finds himself, Brill concocts a story in his imagination, an alternate reality to the reader, reminding one of Tralfamador in <i>Slaughterhouse Five</i>. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1590725328i/29558427._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><b>Paul Auster</b>-image from the NY Times<br><br>His alt-world is thinly made, a USA in which civil war erupted after Bush v Gore. If only. His imaginary protagonist is Corporal Owen Brick, a young magician who finds himself transformed into a soldier, worse, an assassin. The target of this assassination has a certain snake-devouring-it’s-own-tail appeal. Brick has relationships that parallel Brill’s and should serve to illuminate his creator’s reality. But it seemed to me that they did not offer much light.<br><br>Not a lot is made of Brick’s career in alt-world, but Brill refers to himself as a magician in real-world, regarding a difficult emotional balancing act. <br><br>The book almost demands multiple readings in order to peel back the layers. First, there is the surface. Brill is a man who is trying to cope with having suffered a great loss. That makes him sympathetic, garnering our interest. It turns out he is maybe not all that wonderful a person, which certainly loosens our connection. But his affection for his daughter and granddaughter, and his love for his late wife, leave us enough to keep some connection alive. I was most moved when Brill tells his granddaughter of his life with and love for his late wife. <br><br>Another layer is a question of the nature of our reality. Are we more real than the characters in a story? Why could we not, ourselves, be characters in someone else’s story? <blockquote>by putting myself into the story the story becomes real. Or else I become unreal, yet one more figment of my own imagination. </blockquote> Interesting stuff, if a bit like trying to read a popular science piece about string theory. Does that make God the ultimate author? Are we all characters in our own novels? <blockquote>And why does this man deserve to die?<br>Because he owns the war. He invented it, and everything that happens or is about to happen is in his head. Eliminate that head and the war stops. It’s that simple.<br>Simple? You make him sound like God.<br>Not God, Corporal, just a man. He sits in a room all day writing it down, and whatever he writes comes true. </blockquote>There are observations on the state of contemporary life as well, particularly the permanent war of 1984. Brill/Brick has been moving between realities:<blockquote>How long has it been since you’ve been back on this side?<br>Fifteen years. This is my first trip. It wasn’t even possible until about three months ago. You were the first one to go back and forth. Did you know that?<br>No one ever told me anything. <br>It’s like stepping into a dream, isn’t it? The same place, but entirely different. America without war. It’s hard to digest. You get so used to the fighting, it kind of creeps into your bones, and after a while, you can’t imagine a world without it.<br>America’s at war. All right. We’re just not fighting it here. Not yet, anyway. </blockquote>Wars figure prominently in the tale. There is the civil war in alt-world, the Iraq war in real-world, history told about World War II, and on a smaller scale, one conflict stands in for larger ones: <blockquote>[In Newark during the 1967 riot] Not long after we returned to the mayor's office, in walked a member of the New Jersey State police, a certain Colonel Brand or Brandt, a man around forty with a razor-sharp crew cut, a square, clenched jaw and the hard eyes of a marine about to embark on a commando mission. He shook hands with [mayor] Addonizio, sat down in a chair, and then pronounced these words: We're gonna hunt down every black bastard in this city. I probably shouldn't have been shocked, but I was. Not by the statement, perhaps, but by the chilling contempt of the voice that uttered it...that was my war. Not a real war, perhaps, but once you witness violence on that scale, it isn't difficult to imagine something worse, and once your mind is capable of doing that, you understand that the worst possibilities of the imagination are the country you live in. Just think it, and the chances are it will happen. </blockquote>Auster has some fun with names. The Molly Wald in alt-world is hardly the sweetheart of John Hughes fame. That Brill asks his granddaughter to call him Augie can hardly be pure coincidence. He tosses in references to Blake and Bloch as well.<br><br>Brill and Katya self-medicate to some classic foreign films, noting particularly how some directors use inanimate objects to convey emotional content. This leads one to look for that in the novel, but if it was present, I missed it. <br><br>I am not sure I liked this book, but I found it very thought-provoking, and may even go back and read it again. I got the feeling that there was much here that did not get through my dense hide.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7482546863040562452" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11067486330923004376"><i>
  <b>“I am alone in the dark.”</b>
</i><br/><br/>I'd never read a book by Auster before.<br/>I'd heard he had deeply devoted fans, and now I know why. This guy's in a class by himself.<br/><br/>Do you want to read something unlike anything you've ever read before? Read this one.<br/><br/>A man wakes up in a deep, dark hole in the ground.   <span id="freeText11067486330923004376" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>“I am alone in the dark.”</b>
</i><br /><br />I'd never read a book by Auster before.<br />I'd heard he had deeply devoted fans, and now I know why. This guy's in a class by himself.<br /><br />Do you want to read something unlike anything you've ever read before? Read this one.<br /><br />A man wakes up in a deep, dark hole in the ground. He doesn't know where he is or how he got there. He can hear the sounds of warfare outside.<br /><br />He has woken up in a parallel world in a parallel America. This America is caught in the throws of a tragic second civil war. But that's not the whole story, not even close.<br /><br /><i>“There's no single reality, Corporal. There are many realities. There's no single world. There are many worlds, and they all run parallel to one another, worlds and anti-worlds, worlds and shadow-worlds, and each world is dreamed or imagined or written by someone in another world. Each world is the creation of a mind.”</i><br /><br />This book is strange, it's different, it's great. It's Auster.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer328947838" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13823203431589121507">Man in the Dark, Paul Auster<br/>Man in the Dark is a novel by Paul Auster published in August 2008. Its topic is a dystopian scenario of the present-day United States being torn apart by a new secession and civil war after the presidential elections of 2000. (The fictional division between the secessionist and loyal states is very similar to the "Jesusland" map.) This is told within a frame narrative of an aging journalist reflecting on his family and the death of his wife.<br/>تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بی</span>
  <span id="freeText13823203431589121507" style="display:none">Man in the Dark, Paul Auster<br />Man in the Dark is a novel by Paul Auster published in August 2008. Its topic is a dystopian scenario of the present-day United States being torn apart by a new secession and civil war after the presidential elections of 2000. (The fictional division between the secessionist and loyal states is very similar to the "Jesusland" map.) This is told within a frame narrative of an aging journalist reflecting on his family and the death of his wife.<br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و سوم ماه مارس سال 2009 میلادی<br />عنوان: مردی در تاریکی؛ پل آستر؛ مترجم: خجسته کیهان؛ تهران، افق، 1388، در 218 ص، شابک: 9789643695941؛<br />عنوان: مرد در تاریکی؛ پل آستر؛ مترجم: الاهه دهنوی؛ تهران، مروارید، 1388، در 193 ص، شابک: 9789641910374؛<br />رمان «مردی در تاریکی»، داستان نویسنده ای ست، که روی صندلی چرخدارش بنشسته، و در خیالش قصه ای را شکل میدهد، که شخصیتهای آن، دل خوشی، از آفریننده اش ندارند، و با استخدام مردی در جهان بیرون داستان، قصد کشتن او را دارند. در این داستان جهان واقعی (یا واقعیت بیرونی) با شکل خاصی با جهان ذهنی نویسنده اش، به هم تنیده میشود و استر با استادی تمام سعی میکند تا به ورطه ی کلیشه های موجود در چنین داستانهایی نیفتد. آستر نویسنده نام آشنای ادبیات پسامدرن، که همواره با نگاهی فلسفی به زندگی و ذات بشر قلم زده است؛ اینبار به جای هویتیابی فردی، و پرداختن به وضعیت شخصی، به وضعیت سیاسی جامعه ی آمریکا، میپردازد. ا. شربیانی</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_118006920" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/118006920" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/118006920?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312428518" href="/book/show/6413106-man-in-the-dark">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      Paul Auster fans
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=saddest">saddest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=war">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer118006920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6457427810912803003">The Philippines exports lots of domestic helpers (household helpers, nannies, girl Fridays, cleaners, caregivers, etc.) to overseas. Those ladies and men are normally college graduates or finished some units in college. Most of them are teachers because their monthly salary here in the Philippines is low and not enough to satisfy that they think they families deserve. Most of them find their possible employers from agencies who have contacts abroad, mostly in Hong Kong, Singapore and some countr</span>
  <span id="freeText6457427810912803003" style="display:none">The Philippines exports lots of domestic helpers (household helpers, nannies, girl Fridays, cleaners, caregivers, etc.) to overseas. Those ladies and men are normally college graduates or finished some units in college. Most of them are teachers because their monthly salary here in the Philippines is low and not enough to satisfy that they think they families deserve. Most of them find their possible employers from agencies who have contacts abroad, mostly in Hong Kong, Singapore and some countries in the Middle East, Europe and North America.<br /><br />Had I been one of them, I would apply to be a domestic helper in America. But I will be choosy in which household I want to be employed at. After all, I also went to a graduate school on top of my double-college degrees! <br /><br />And the first in my list will be the residence of Mr. Paul Auster, 64, and his wife Ms. Siri Hustvedt, 55! <br /><br />This is my fourth book by Sir Paul Auster and I guess he has no way to disappoint me. <i>New York Trilogy</i> (1987), <i>The Music of Chance</i> (1990) and <i>Invisible</i> (2009). They all got 4 stars from me. His works are not mushy and melodramatic yet they are sentimental and they can trigger a flurry of emotions. They have enough suspense and his characters are enigmatic. His imagination is superbly wild and you’ll never able to predict where he is bringing you to. That character-driven suspense that is different from the usual plot-driven suspense, i.e., your <i>standard</i> mystery-thriller style, is what makes Paul Auster’s trademark different from others in this genre.<br /><br />And his wife, his second, the one who lives with him now: Ma’am Siri Hustvedt, I’ve read two of her books, <i>The Enchantment of Lily Dahl</i> (1996) and <i>What I Loved</i> (2003) and they are both surprisingly brilliant! Her prose is a bit dark, her storytelling is subtle yet the imagery that she creates and her characters are all multi-dimensional. They are not mushy too that is a breath of fresh air from the many female writers of her (or our) generation.<br /><br />I would like to imagine myself in their household washing their dishes and listening to their husband-wife conversations. Huh?!? Not the bedroom conversations, you and your dirty mind... Or me dusting off their volumes and volumes of books in their library and secretly reading their manuscripts in their work desks. That would be the best job in the world! Oh, Sir Paul Auster and Ma’am Siri Hustvedt, hire me, please? I am willing to wipe off your asses when you are already too old to do it yourselves!<br /><br /> <i>Man in the Dark</i> my 4th novel written by Sir Paul Auster was first published in 2008. There is nothing really original about it. I’ve already read books with “a story within a story” like Italo Calvino’s <i>If on a Winter’s Night a Traveler</i> (1979) or even “a story within a story within a story” like Flann O’Brien’s <i>At Swim-To-Birds</i> (1939). The mysterious man in the hole also is not original since Haruki Murakami had his Toru Okada in the hole in his 1994 masterpiece, <i>The Wind-Up Bird Chronicle.</i><br /><br />This book is also a bit different from Sir Paul Auster’s usual mystery-thriller flavor. It does not have any character who is a detective or with mysterious background or past. However, you would still feel the mystery behind his characters by reading their thoughts, expressed or implied. This book starts with this line narrated by the main protagonist, a 72-y/o <b>August Brill</b> who could not sleep because of insomnia: <i> "I am alone in the dark, turning the world around in my head as I struggle through another bout of insomnia, another white night in the great American wilderness."</i> Brill’s wife, <b>Sonia</b> recently died of cancer and he is recuperating from a car accident. He could not sleep so he tells himself stories. The stories seem to come alive as his life later becomes intertwined with the fictional characters that he himself created. Well, I’ll not go into details because that would be too much of a spoiler.<br /><br />The ending of the story within the story is just too abrupt; I found it a bit jarring. I wished Sir Paul Auster continued and made it a bit longer.<br /><br />Also, the oft-repeated line, <i>
  <b>‘the weird world rolls on,</b>
</i>’ apparently penned by Rose Hawthorne, the daughter of Nathaniel (of <i>The House of the Seven Gables</i>), is the novel’s leitmotif. But cute as the phrase is, Sir Paul Auster <i>sorry, Sir!</i> seems to send a pessimistic message at the end of the book. <br /><br />The book has everything good in it except a proper positive ending. But sir, ma’am, when will I send my resume? I am all-around. I can cook, I can scrub your floor, I know how to use the washing machine, I can drive, I know how to take your blood pressure, I can tend to your garden, I know how to vacuum, I can wash your cars, I am fond of dogs, etc etc. I can even work for free if you only let me read your manuscripts!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6457427810912803003" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2435181725" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2435181725" />
    <a title="Sohaib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib"><img alt="Sohaib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599511226p2/31777090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2435181725?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sohaib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sohaib" href="/user/show/31777090-sohaib">Sohaib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312428518" href="/book/show/6413106-man-in-the-dark">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=2018-reads">2018-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=post-postmodernism">post-postmodernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31777090-sohaib?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2435181725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17883213668438579602">This book made a lasting impression by making me laugh. A full burst of laughing out loud. No book has ever done that for me. <br/><br/>I'm including the funny part by the end of this review. <br/><br/><i>Man in the Dark</i> tells the story—the stories I should say—of August Brill, a seventy-two-year-old retired literary critic, handicapped and suffering from insomnia. August whiles away the night tossing and turning in bed, thinking up stories either from memory or imagination. <br/><br/>During this long night, he tells us a made-</span>
  <span id="freeText17883213668438579602" style="display:none">This book made a lasting impression by making me laugh. A full burst of laughing out loud. No book has ever done that for me. <br /><br />I'm including the funny part by the end of this review. <br /><br /><i>Man in the Dark</i> tells the story—the stories I should say—of August Brill, a seventy-two-year-old retired literary critic, handicapped and suffering from insomnia. August whiles away the night tossing and turning in bed, thinking up stories either from memory or imagination. <br /><br />During this long night, he tells us a made-up story about some Owen Brick, a man who wakes up in a hole in a fictional America torn by a second civil war. The story happens in August's mind, and the war reflects his inner turmoil and inability to sleep.<br /><br />Brick, our metafictional hero, is suddenly rescued by a certain Tobak, a sergeant who comes in with a rope and an intel: Brick's mission is to end the war by killing the only one responsible, August Freaking Brill. <br /><br />At this point, the whole thing seems like an unconscious defense mechanism on the part of August—an embedded fantasy inside the main narrative that conceals his unconscious desire to die, or a displacing of his desire to kill himself by projecting it on a fictional character. This is what the Freudians call Thanatos. <br /><br />Anyway, this narrative scheme fits in with the post-postmodern style and everything mellows out into a heartwarming familial tale of love and compassion, an empathic attempt to relate—a deep seated desire for human connection and warmth. The whole thing just turns out in a beautiful way. <br /><br />I say this but I've forgotten to mention some important details about the reasons of August's insomnia. He lives with his daughter Miriam, a forty-seven-year-old writer who has been abandoned by her husband, and her daughter Katya, a twenty-three-year-old former student transformed into a shut-in after the gruesome killing of her boyfriend in Iraq. <br /><br />The book ends with a communal sharing of love and relatedness, foreshadowing a coming to inner peace and harmony with the beautiful voice of Rose Hawthorne saying, <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"As the weird world rolls on."</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />Full-blown five stars and favorited.<br /><br />—————————————————————————<br /><br />And yeah, this is the funny part:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"My mother had a voluminous chest, twenty times the size of Sonia's. One Saturday morning, she and Miriam were sitting on the sofa together watching cartoons. A commercial for pizza came on, which ended with the words: Now, that's a pizza! A moment later, your mother turned to my mother, clamped her mouth on her grandmother’s right breast, and then came up shouting: Now, that’s a pizza! My mother laughed so hard, she let out a fart, a gigantic trumpet blast of a fart. That got Miriam laughing so wildly, she peed in her pants. She jumped off the sofa and started running around the room, yelling at the top of her lungs: Fart-pee, fart-pee, oui, oui, oui!"</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />I just couldn't help but lose it when the great grandmother farted :D<br /><br />—————————————————————————</span>
  <a data-text-id="17883213668438579602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3593105733554594037">For me a novel by Paul Auster always is a winner!!!<br/><br/>The writing is superb, the story flows unconstrained, casually and easily like as propel by magic..<br/>The characters brimming with life, unmistakable it is the powerful voice of one of the few remaining master storyteller..<br/>Indeed a true American original!!<br/><br/>Well having said that, "The Man in the Dark" kept me turning the pages and if it does exists something called a compulsive reading, then here you have it before your nose!!<br/><br/>Honestly folks, I litera</span>
  <span id="freeText3593105733554594037" style="display:none">For me a novel by Paul Auster always is a winner!!!<br /><br />The writing is superb, the story flows unconstrained, casually and easily like as propel by magic..<br />The characters brimming with life, unmistakable it is the powerful voice of one of the few remaining master storyteller..<br />Indeed a true American original!!<br /><br />Well having said that, "The Man in the Dark" kept me turning the pages and if it does exists something called a compulsive reading, then here you have it before your nose!!<br /><br />Honestly folks, I literally couldn't put it down..<br />If you wanted to have my hardback edition then you had to tear it out of my dead and cold hands..<br /><br />From the dust jacket:<br />"Passionate and shocking.. a book that forces us to confront the darkness off night even as it celebrates the existence of ordinary joys.."<br /><br />Seventy-two-year-old August Brill is a Literatur critic, after having had a bad car accident he tries to recover from and night after night he tells himself stories..<br /><br />He has to dealt with the recent death of his wife Sonia..<br />In Vermont living with his daughter and granddaughter the last decisive struggle of his life is lash loose hunted by memories and the demons of the past!!!<br /><br />Parallel worlds, the negative and eroding force of guilt, the vulnerability of trusting, and the deep and bitter wounds of betrayal, this are some of the topics dealt in this novel..<br /><br />A book which everyone should read..<br />Pregnant and saturated with the power of change our awareness, conscience, and realization!!!<br /><br />Happy readings<br /><br />Dean;)<br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2417321697?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2417321697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11063526988158986472">او دست آخر به غمگین‌ترین فردی تبدیل شد که دیده‌ام. در بهار سال 1987 یک روز صبح خدمتکارش وحشت‌زده به من تلفن کرد. با کلیدی که برای نظافت هفتگی در اختیارش گذاشته بود وارد آپارتمان شده و جسد خواهرم را روی تختخواب یافته بود. اتومبیل همسایه را قرض گرفتم، تا نیوجرسی راندم و هویت جسد را برای پلیس شناسایی کردم. دیدن او در آن حال بسیار شوک‌آور بود: چنان بی‌حرکت، دور و مرده، حقیقتا مرده. وقتی پرسیدند مایلم جسد را برای کالبدشکافی بفرستند، گفتم آن را به حال خود بگذارند. تنها دو امکان وجود داشت: یا به صورت ط</span>
  <span id="freeText11063526988158986472" style="display:none">او دست آخر به غمگین‌ترین فردی تبدیل شد که دیده‌ام. در بهار سال 1987 یک روز صبح خدمتکارش وحشت‌زده به من تلفن کرد. با کلیدی که برای نظافت هفتگی در اختیارش گذاشته بود وارد آپارتمان شده و جسد خواهرم را روی تختخواب یافته بود. اتومبیل همسایه را قرض گرفتم، تا نیوجرسی راندم و هویت جسد را برای پلیس شناسایی کردم. دیدن او در آن حال بسیار شوک‌آور بود: چنان بی‌حرکت، دور و مرده، حقیقتا مرده. وقتی پرسیدند مایلم جسد را برای کالبدشکافی بفرستند، گفتم آن را به حال خود بگذارند. تنها دو امکان وجود داشت: یا به صورت طبیعی از بین رفته بود، یا با خوردن مقداری قرص خودکشی کرده بود، و من نمی‌خواستم پاسخ را بدانم، زیرا هیچ یک واقعیت ماجرا را برملا نمی‌کردند. بتی بر اثر شکستن دلش مرده بود. شنیدن این اصطلاح برای بعضی‌ها خنده‌آور است، اما به این دلیل که چیزی از دنیا نمی‌دانند. آدم‌ها از دل شکستگی می‌میرند. این چیزی است که هر روز اتفاق می‌افتد و تا آخر زمان هم روی خواهد داد.</span>

<div id="review_24920268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24920268" />
    <a title="Imogen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33887-imogen"><img alt="Imogen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353870889p2/33887.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24920268?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Imogen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Imogen" href="/user/show/33887-imogen">Imogen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24920268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13274200376241083135">Back when I was an undergraduate in college (not that I ever did any graduate work, but I'm about to make fun of myself, and making fun of 'undergraduates' is a literary tradition in these parts) I got a total boner* for structuralism. And then post-structuralism. I was dating Sarah and she was like, 'Hey, you like this soulless pomo bullshit, you should read this book I just read and didn't like, the New York Trilogy,' I was all, sweet, empty soulless pomo bullshit! And read it, and didn't real</span>
  <span id="freeText13274200376241083135" style="display:none">Back when I was an undergraduate in college (not that I ever did any graduate work, but I'm about to make fun of myself, and making fun of 'undergraduates' is a literary tradition in these parts) I got a total boner* for structuralism. And then post-structuralism. I was dating Sarah and she was like, 'Hey, you like this soulless pomo bullshit, you should read this book I just read and didn't like, the New York Trilogy,' I was all, sweet, empty soulless pomo bullshit! And read it, and didn't really like it much, and realized that- as we learned from Alain Robbe-Grillet- fiction rooted in structuralist or post-structuralist theory is way more fun to talk about than it is to read. <br /><br />So anyway, I was all, fuck Paul Auster, that guy writes boring experimental novels that don't make me have any feelings. Y'know, judging his twenty or so books on the basis of one early work. And then person after person who I respected- and probably more relevantly, who could give a fuck about Roland Barthes- were like, 'oh man, that Paul Auster book rules, it's told from the perspective of a dog' or 'dude the Brooklyn Follies is the best book I've ever read' or 'man I get so hopeless when I read Paul Auster.'<br /><br />Which always perks up my jaded ears. Hopeless? Yeah? <br /><br />So a proof of his new book came into the store, it was short, and I said, what the fuck. Why not. And it was good! Turns out I've been an asshole the whole time, which I love to find out. I think it'd be hard to make a five-star book out of a hundred and eighty pages, but that's just me- this book worked really well. It approached metafiction but never got to the heights of plasticity and masturbation that that word implies to me- it was kind of self-referential, but only insofar as the protagonist was referring to himself for himself. Or whatever. The point is, three quarters of the way through- um, spoiler- the Auster just kind of abandons the whole metafiction conceit and tells a story. <br /><br />Which, I mean, let's metaphorize- are we talking about metafictionalyzing as a way to avoid telling direct emotional truths? And is this book then kind of a refutation of the experimentalism of the New York Trilogy? Are you saying, Paul Auster, that I was right not to like your signature work much? Or is this just a bunch more postmodern redirection and shuffling of signs?<br /><br />Either way, as a story, this works on its own, and in the context of what I know of Auster's work (which like I said isn't much) it's pretty friggin interesting. So yes! It felt like there wasn't quite enough novel to give it five stars, but it's definitely worth four. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />*How in love with the phrase 'total boner' am I lately? Jesus, I feel like I use it once or twice in every review, like back when I started every single one with 'maaaaaaaan...'</span>
  <a data-text-id="13274200376241083135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_255001145" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/255001145" />
    <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1"><img alt="Shovelmonkey1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391713268p2/3926982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/255001145?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shovelmonkey1" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571240777" href="/book/show/6535360-man-in-the-dark">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who like their words carefully folded</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Shovelmonkey1 by: </span>
      a previous brief encounter with Auster
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=bookcrossing-books">bookcrossing-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer255001145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7384989978328767649">*Fanfare*<br/>Yes, it is Paul Auster time again ladies and gentlemen. <br/><br/>Paul. I love you. <br/><br/>Almost as hard to write as it is to say, although technically my fingers are more limber and better versed at communication as they are without the subtle patina of sceptical hoar frost which coats my cryogenically chilled cardio-vascular chambers. <br/><br/>As I have previously discussed, my first few dates with Paul were suspicious, sullen affairs where I peered at the pages with an arched eyebrow trying to decided... " g</span>
  <span id="freeText7384989978328767649" style="display:none">*Fanfare*<br />Yes, it is Paul Auster time again ladies and gentlemen. <br /><br />Paul. I love you. <br /><br />Almost as hard to write as it is to say, although technically my fingers are more limber and better versed at communication as they are without the subtle patina of sceptical hoar frost which coats my cryogenically chilled cardio-vascular chambers. <br /><br />As I have previously discussed, my first few dates with Paul were suspicious, sullen affairs where I peered at the pages with an arched eyebrow trying to decided... " genius or arty-toss?" There was no love, oh no. For a while I was swerving towards arty-toss, then actually I realised just how wrong I was. And then there was the love, oh yes.<br /><br />Man in the Dark treads a familiar but not unwelcome path with a story, within a story, within a story but not in the David Mitchell Cloud Atlas-y five tale matrioshka way. This is more subtle - more intricately folded than conveniently stacked. A Man in the Dark, alone, lulls himself to sleep by telling a fictional tale of a parallel world where America did not go to war with Iraq, instead it went to war with itself. For this man a re-imagining of the past helps him come to terms with the present for there is another story which does not want to be told. <br /><br />Paul Auster is the master of literary origami and I am a big fan of the way his stories unfold. They unfold and unfolds and oh look, it's a crane. But wait, if you keep unfolding it becomes one of those weird six sided fortune telling things that you used to make in the playground when you were a kid. You know the kind I mean? Pick a colour, pick a number... either way it's not the same shaped thing that you started out with but it is still the same book. <br /><br />I have In the Country of Last Things lined up to be read this month. Expect more love and possibly more bad origami references.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7384989978328767649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_33595664" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33595664" />
    <a title="Kitty-Wu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1880-kitty-wu"><img alt="Kitty-Wu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599132237p2/1880.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33595664?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kitty-Wu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kitty-Wu" href="/user/show/1880-kitty-wu">Kitty-Wu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788433973641" href="/book/show/4716189-un-hombre-en-la-oscuridad">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33595664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2976054367608985608">Me da igual que Auster empiece a jugar a las muñecas rusas en la página 3, me da igual que me recuerde a Travels in the Scriptorium, me da igual que sus personajes me recuerden a otros, me da igual que haya tanto escritor suelto en Nueva York, me da absolutamente igual todo, porque una vez más, Auster me engancha desde la primera página y no me deja ir. Y estoy tan a gusto dentro de su mundo... el otro día salía por esta página el tema de nuestros autores vivos favoritos: bien, yo me iría de cop</span>
  <span id="freeText2976054367608985608" style="display:none">Me da igual que Auster empiece a jugar a las muñecas rusas en la página 3, me da igual que me recuerde a Travels in the Scriptorium, me da igual que sus personajes me recuerden a otros, me da igual que haya tanto escritor suelto en Nueva York, me da absolutamente igual todo, porque una vez más, Auster me engancha desde la primera página y no me deja ir. Y estoy tan a gusto dentro de su mundo... el otro día salía por esta página el tema de nuestros autores vivos favoritos: bien, yo me iría de copas con Nick Hornby, sin dudarlo. Me hubiera gustado tener un affaire adolescente con Baricco, de ésos medio platónicos, con vino, rosas y poemas. Me hubiera encantado que Benedetti fuera mi abuelo. Y tener de profesor a Camus, aunque no este vivo, hubiera sido lo máximo. Incluso me gustaría una charla con Palahniuk, aunque estaría toda ella sufriendo. Y Stephen King hubiera sido un compañero de juegos genial en la infancia y preadolescencia. <br>Pero para compartir mi vida adulta, quiero a Auster. Quiero su inteligencia sin pedantería, quiero sus historias dentro de historias, quiero su sinceridad sin afectación, quiero su tristeza y su infinita curiosidad... quiero compartir más cosas con él. Y por eso, aunque vivo en Barcelona y cada año por Sant Jordi podría ir a que mis autores favoritos me firmaran sus libros, nunca lo hecho. Y sin embargo, nada me impedirá estar en el Saló de Cent del Ayuntamiento de Barcelona el 6 de Octubre para poder contemplar a Mr. Auster in person.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1422879963i/13557948._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br></span>

<div id="review_2745189348" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2745189348" />
    <a title="Nour Allam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56203546-nour-allam"><img alt="Nour Allam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602334821p2/56203546.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2745189348?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nour Allam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nour Allam" href="/user/show/56203546-nour-allam">Nour Allam</a>

<div id="review_1438996242" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1438996242" />
    <a title="João Carlos" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17544299-jo-o-carlos"><img alt="João Carlos" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528787185p2/17544299.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1438996242?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="João Carlos" class="user" itemprop="url" name="João Carlos" href="/user/show/17544299-jo-o-carlos">João Carlos</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789892302669" href="/book/show/6324177-homem-na-escurid-o">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17544299-jo-o-carlos?shelf=l200pag">l200pag</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17544299-jo-o-carlos?shelf=l2018">l2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17544299-jo-o-carlos?shelf=paul-auster">paul-auster</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1438996242" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5965265085002798810"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1540207366i/26483224.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><b>Paul Auster</b> (n. 1947) pelo ilustrador português <b>André Carrilho</b><br/><br/><b>”Homem na Escuridão”</b> (2008) é o décimo segundo livro que li do escritor norte-americano <b>Paul Auster</b> (n. 1947). <br/>Com setenta e dois anos de idade August Brill, um crítico literário reformado, está a viver nos arredores de Brattleboro, Vermont na casa da sua filha única Miriam, com quarenta e sete anos e com a sua neta Katya, de vinte e três anos, uma obsessiva cinéfila, que abandonou a escola de cinema em Nova Yorque, em consequência de</span>
  <span id="freeText5965265085002798810" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1540207366i/26483224.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Paul Auster</b> (n. 1947) pelo ilustrador português <b>André Carrilho</b><br><br><b>”Homem na Escuridão”</b> (2008) é o décimo segundo livro que li do escritor norte-americano <b>Paul Auster</b> (n. 1947). <br>Com setenta e dois anos de idade August Brill, um crítico literário reformado, está a viver nos arredores de Brattleboro, Vermont na casa da sua filha única Miriam, com quarenta e sete anos e com a sua neta Katya, de vinte e três anos, uma obsessiva cinéfila, que abandonou a escola de cinema em Nova Yorque, em consequência de um acontecimento trágico; a sua mulher, Sonia, morreu de cancro. <br>August Brill está sozinho na escuridão com mais uma insónia. Há mais de um ano a tentar recuperar de um acidente de viação, perscruta o negro das noites, conta histórias a si próprio e vê e revê com Katya: <b>”A Grande Ilusão”</b>, <b>”Ladrões de Bicicletas”</b> e <b>”O Mundo de Apu”</b>.<br>Na(s) sua(s) história(s) ou no seu livro, August Brill, conta que Owen Brick está num buraco cilíndrico e não sabe como foi lá parar. Para sua grande surpresa enverga um uniforme militar. Nesta América as Torres Gémeas não caíram, mas as eleições presidenciais de 2000 conduziram a uma guerra de secessão, com os estados a abandonar a união. <br>August Brill é assombrado por pensamentos dramáticos. <br><b>Paul Auster</b> constrói o romance em vários níveis narrativos conjugando as influências literárias e as influências cinematográficas, com uma obsessão – a guerra. Apesar de o conflito poder ser imaginário, a desintegração do território americano revela uma inquietude efectiva e realista. <br><b>”Homem na Escuridão”</b> é um romance engenhoso na construção – um livro dentro de um livro -, uma obra de memórias dolorosas, numa narrativa inteligente e original, uma excelente meditação sobre a diferença entre as histórias que queremos contar e as histórias que acabamos contando, com personagens impregnadas de tristeza e de dor. <br><b>”Homem na Escuridão”</b> é igualmente um somatório de diversas histórias que se entrelaçam; o arrependimento e a culpabilidade surgem do confronto entre a perda e o remorso. A ansiedade das personagens deriva não só da sua solidão mas também dos equívocos dos seus actos. <br><b>”Luz radiosa, depois escuridão. O sol que se derrama de todos os cantos do céu, seguido pela escuridão da noite, as estrelas silenciosas, o vento que se agita nos ramos. Tal é a rotina.”</b> (Pág. 7) – este parágrafo é apenas um exemplo da mestria e do controlo da escrita e da narrativa que tanto me tem atraído nos romances de <b>Paul Auster</b>. <br><b>Paul Auster</b> é um escritor imprescindível.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1504647626" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1504647626" />
    <a title="Joey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10049704-joey"><img alt="Joey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493309397p2/10049704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1504647626?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey" href="/user/show/10049704-joey">Joey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1504647626" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7635220106171571847">It is weird that when I see Paul Auster’s works included on the list of best novels of all time according to a magazine site, I have the compelling hunch that they are nifty reads. Also, when I see his images on Google, his physical aura of literary skills , his deep stare at the camera tends to pierce me as if everything stops moving just like the two of us in a motionless world. It is as though staring back at him renders me powerless ,frozen in awe. Thereby, I start having been borne upon the</span>
  <span id="freeText7635220106171571847" style="display:none">It is weird that when I see Paul Auster’s works included on the list of best novels of all time according to a magazine site, I have the compelling hunch that they are nifty reads. Also, when I see his images on Google, his physical aura of literary skills , his deep stare at the camera tends to pierce me as if everything stops moving just like the two of us in a motionless world. It is as though staring back at him renders me powerless ,frozen in awe. Thereby, I start having been borne upon the idea that whenever I spot his books heaped on a mountain of books, there is a feeling that the author is a sacred cow to whom every book vulture should pay homage. And me? I am wildly and outrageously glad to jump at them as if I should kowtow to them even if all book vultures milling around the place cringe at my losing sense of decorum. What do they know? They may be in the dark that the books I long to gorge myself on are freshly nutritious. How do I know? They may not know that I have wolfed on one of his works- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/449.Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu by Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">Timbuktu</a>. ( The title has nothing to do with the title of my blog.) The book gave me the appetite that Auster is a gifted writer. There is something in his style that left a good aftertaste in my mouth then. So, no need to wonder why perhaps I am one of those book vultures who bear that desire to scavenger on his other works. <br /><br />In his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/449.Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu by Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">Timbuktu</a>, the first thing I noticed was his light sentence structure- very well-written and prosy. At the same time, the concept of the story is philosophically interesting. I apologize for the spoiler. Timbuktu is a dog who has deeply intimate relationship with a hard-pressed, terminally-ill writer. At the end , I assure that you will find it heart- breaking . Alas, I never got the chance to write my review of it ; at that time , I still was not active on Goodreads and was ignorant of blogging. You may find the story common, for you have seen it in movies or TV dramas, but you will be amazed at what I call ‘ Auster’s simply brilliant work’. If you have not started reading his other works yet, I believe that Timbuktu is the springboard for discovering his talent. Go for it!<br /><br />Now I have given a try at his Man in the Dark. I was a little astonished to find out that his writing style in this novel bears complete resemblance to his Timbuktu. I do not have the foggiest idea if his other works do likewise. Here I felt the lightness of his sentence structures, how he must choose the right words, phrases, or sentence structures ditto. So I enjoyed reading the novel without cease, without putting it aside if there were odds and ends I had to futz around first. When I was done with them, I would throw myself into it forgetting the world I was in. No wonder I did finish it all at once given the fact that it only consists of 180 pages. <br /><br />Concept of the story:<br />August Brill is a seventy-two-year-old widower. He recovers from a car accident at his daughter’s house in Vermont. To kill time, he watches films which he criticizes since he is a retired book critic. He does it with his granddaughter who has the same interest. When he cannot sleep, he lies in bed in the dark staring into the ceiling and trying to tell himself stories. At the same time, in doing so, he cannot remember his wife and the heinous murder of his granddaughter’s boyfriend, Titus.<br /><br />I may be familiar with the setting that there is “a minor story in the story”, but for me, I do not look at that perspective; rather, I find the essence of the story mind-boggling. For instance, what is the relevance to the dystopian settings that the World Trade did not fall apart, that the U.S did not fight with Iran, instead the 2000 election results caused secession, that the state after state pulled away from the union and a bloody civil war broke out? I mused over this essence, on the way to work by bus, during my 10-minute break in school, or even during my processing inside a john. That is why it took me a few days to review it. Unfortunately, I was at my wits’ ends. Sorry, folks, I even have my hands full. Maybe you could help me squeeze it out of me. You may claim it not to be a brain surgery at all. ^^ Anyway, I may come to that literary epiphany sometime in the future. For this reason, therefore, Auster injected this enigmatic idea into this story that only he could expound what those ideas in question mean all about. Indeed, he is remarkable. I wish I had attended his launching this book ( Man in the Dark ) if I were American. In a pig’s eye!<br /><br />Given that I found “the minor story” somewhat bothering, I could not divert my emotional attention from the main character’s role which may be the crucial part of the story. In that part, I immersed myself , feeling my tears welling up in the cups of my eyes, reminding me of two people whom I deeply love: my mother who already departed the world and my father, a widower too, whom I have been cold with. Likewise, in the end, it is all about life, life, life as famous writer Ethan Hawthorne’s sister Rose Hawthorne put it, “ As the weird world rolls on.”<br /><br />Now I have devoured two of Auster’s books , although I am still assimilating their substance thoroughly, when I visit the heap of books in the mountain, I will not hesitate to scrounge on his other works, notably <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50618.The_Book_of_Illusions" title="The Book of Illusions by Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Illusions</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/431.The_New_York_Trilogy" title="The New York Trilogy by Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">The New York Trilogy</a>. I can’t wait for them! ^^<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7635220106171571847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2155660302" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9743412689811646179"><b><br/>"وحيدا في الظلام أقلب في رأسي العالم من حولي بينما أغالب نوبة أرق جديدة, ليلية بيضاء جديدة في البراري الأمريكية الشاسعة"<br/><br/>ثلاثة أشخاص يمثلون ثلاثة أجيال مختلفة, يجمع بينهم إلى جانب العلاقة الأسرية إنتماهم لعالم من الوحدة و الإنكسار. لكأن بول أوستير يحاول أن يقول لنا من خلال هذه الرواية : حتى و لو كنت على بعد آلاف الأميال لا بد أن تصل إليك الحرب و تقرع باب بيتك, و حتى و إن كنت في واقع آخر لا بد للولايات المتحدة أن تخوض حربا و إن ضد نفسها. <br/>الرواية عميقة جدا لكن ما أزعجني هي النهاية المفاجئة لقصة بري</b></span>
  <span id="freeText9743412689811646179" style="display:none"><b><br />"وحيدا في الظلام أقلب في رأسي العالم من حولي بينما أغالب نوبة أرق جديدة, ليلية بيضاء جديدة في البراري الأمريكية الشاسعة"<br /><br />ثلاثة أشخاص يمثلون ثلاثة أجيال مختلفة, يجمع بينهم إلى جانب العلاقة الأسرية إنتماهم لعالم من الوحدة و الإنكسار. لكأن بول أوستير يحاول أن يقول لنا من خلال هذه الرواية : حتى و لو كنت على بعد آلاف الأميال لا بد أن تصل إليك الحرب و تقرع باب بيتك, و حتى و إن كنت في واقع آخر لا بد للولايات المتحدة أن تخوض حربا و إن ضد نفسها. <br />الرواية عميقة جدا لكن ما أزعجني هي النهاية المفاجئة لقصة بريل هكذا دون مقدمة تذكر. هي قراءتي الأولى لبول أوستر و قراءتي الأولى لأدب أمريكي كتب <br />حديثا بعيدا عن روايات الإثارة , الألغاز و الرومنسية. تجربة ماتعة بحق و سأرغب بتكرارها مجددا بكل تأكيد<br /></b></span>

<div id="review_390895980" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/390895980" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2569035-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455213348p2/2569035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/390895980?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/2569035-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571240777" href="/book/show/6535360-man-in-the-dark">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2569035-mark?shelf=first-person-narration">first-person-narration</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer390895980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13983055128588208825">There are some books which I find seem unpromising but quickly catch you up and sweep you along and this is one of them. An old man, August Brill, newly widowed and bed-ridden owing to a car accident lies unable to sleep as does his daughter, a writer discarded by her husband; and his granddaughter, newly bereaved after the violent death of her ex-boyfriend. In order to pass the bleak, black time of the depths of night he tells himself stories. Stories in which he desperately tries to close his </span>
  <span id="freeText13983055128588208825" style="display:none">There are some books which I find seem unpromising but quickly catch you up and sweep you along and this is one of them. An old man, August Brill, newly widowed and bed-ridden owing to a car accident lies unable to sleep as does his daughter, a writer discarded by her husband; and his granddaughter, newly bereaved after the violent death of her ex-boyfriend. In order to pass the bleak, black time of the depths of night he tells himself stories. Stories in which he desperately tries to close his mind off to the encroaching reality of the sadnesses and heartbreaks which echo and re-echo around the house.<br /><br />The initial long-running story he imagined of a young children's entertainer transported into a parallel universe and given the job of killing the man responsible for creating the story in which there is no twin towers attack and no war with Iraq but instead a full scale civil war between the United States, is bizarre and clever and shocking all at the same time. It reminded me of 'Slaughterhouse 5' in its surreal flight of fancy but here the traveller, unlike Billy Pilgrim, leaps not from world to world but is imprisoned in someone else's imagination. Brill is himself the imaginer of the story and therefore he is imagining the plot of his own assassination. He is desperate to avoid encountering the effects of the War in Iraq for reasons that unfold so he imagines it never happened, he is desperate to avoid the pain of his loneliness and the loneliness of those he loves so he imagines his own death and escape from that pain even only if through a story.<br /><br />Gradually through a long night's conversation with his granddaughter, they both begin to come to terms with the pain and responsibilty in-built into life and its decisions. It is an incredibly moving reflection on love and the implications inherent in the fact that once love has been acknowledged and 'owned' it becomes unstoppable and immeasurable in the sense that it is bestowed unguardedly not counted out in the fashion of misers. It speaks of missed opportunities and squandered chances and the wonder of new life fighting its way through any amount of crap to spear the frozen surface of an icy heart.<br /><br /> The power of imagination is celebrated for good or ill. Katya, the young mourner, watches film after film after film and analyses and rejoices in their ingenuity; she celebrates the ability of the inanimate object to speak long and loud about the emotions of the filmaker and it is only towards the end of the long conversation she has with her grandad that we are confronted as with a brick wall the reaon for this seeming obsession.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[One viewing and recognition of genius can eradicate the horror of her boyfriend's filmed execution <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. August uses his imagination to escape reality but in the end all comes full circle and he sees that reality needs to be faced, truths need to be told and genuine responsibilty needs to be accepted. In this way, and only in this way, " can the weird world roll on".<br /><br />Re-reading I realize that as is so often the case I have not done the book justice but thats why i am a part time reviewer and Auster the writer he is. Nevertheless this is a really beautiful book, very powerful and well worth the time and effort. I leave the last word to Auster.<br /><br />"Books force you to give something back to them, to exercise your intelligence and imagination"</span>
  <a data-text-id="13983055128588208825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_772083176" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/772083176" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/772083176?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312428518" href="/book/show/6413106-man-in-the-dark">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer772083176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9565436632038538684">I enjoy the 'tricks' in Auster's fiction, even if they are repetitive throughout his oeuvre. Though this novel contains none of his usual tricks, Auster seems to explain why he sometimes puts himself into his own works through the words of August Brill, his narrator. Brill is telling himself a story, in the dark, because he can't sleep: <blockquote>
  <i>The story is about a man who must kill the person who created him, and why pretend that I am not that person? By putting myself into the story, the story beco</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9565436632038538684" style="display:none">I enjoy the 'tricks' in Auster's fiction, even if they are repetitive throughout his oeuvre. Though this novel contains none of his usual tricks, Auster seems to explain why he sometimes puts himself into his own works through the words of August Brill, his narrator. Brill is telling himself a story, in the dark, because he can't sleep: <blockquote>
  <i>The story is about a man who must kill the person who created him, and why pretend that I am not that person? By putting myself into the story, the story becomes real. Or else I become unreal, yet one more figment of my own imagination. Either way, the effect is more satisfying, more in harmony with my mood--which is dark, my little ones, as dark as the obsidian night that surrounds me.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />This slim novel is about more than creativity and insomnia. Its musings have more to do with humanity's seemingly insatiable desire for war (the idea that if a certain war was eliminated, up would pop a different one) and the stories we tell ourselves to try to distance ourselves from the horrors. In its ending (giving me a frisson of discomfort, I saw it coming at the first mention of -- no, I won't say it) Auster ends up looking at one of the horrors unblinkingly, a tribute to the other meaning of the title.<br /><br />Perhaps because its themes are executed in a span of relatively few pages, I was reminded (superficially?) of Martin Amis' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/534339.Time_s_Arrow" title="Time's Arrow by Martin Amis" rel="nofollow noopener">Time's Arrow</a>. And the man in the hole in the story-within-the-story helped me understand more fully the possible meanings of characters of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3354.Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">Haruki Murakami</a> (another writer with his own 'tricks') also finding themselves in deep holes in the ground.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9565436632038538684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6715067640958785035" style="display:none">Numa casa convivem três gerações, três pessoas a viver diferentes tipos de luto, mas em profundo sofrimento. Na escuridão, que é intrínseca e extrínseca, Brill, de 72 anos, assiste a filmes antigos com a neta, debate-se com insónias e as recordações da mulher falecida e escreve uma história em que o protagonista parece ter nas mãos o destino do seu criador. <div id="review_273890345" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="عبدالله ناصر" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7480307"><img alt="عبدالله ناصر" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580712285p2/7480307.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273890345?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="عبدالله ناصر" class="user" itemprop="url" name="عبدالله ناصر" href="/user/show/7480307">عبدالله ناصر</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789953891422" href="/book/show/7788675">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273890345" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10233603689886977989"><br/>رجل في الظلام تجاوز السبعين و بساقٍ مكسورة و يحتل الطابق الأرضي و في الأعلى ابنته الأرملة و حفيدته التي فقدت للأبد صديقها مؤخراً . و لأنه يشكو الأرق يعمد إلى سرد الكثير من القصص و اختلاقها و بهذه الطريقة يساعد عقارب الساعة على العمل أو على وجه الدقة يلهو عن مراقبة الوقت .<br/><br/>يفضل العتمة على الضوء المزعج لعينيه ، في كل الأحوال ذاكرة أوغست بريل مضاءة بالكامل . يستيقظ بطل بريل في حفرة لا يمكنه الخروج منها و بعد لأي و يأس يخرجه أحدهم فيجد أمريكا تخوض حرباً و لكنها هذه المرة تخوضها ضد أمريكا نفسها . أوغس</span>
  <span id="freeText10233603689886977989" style="display:none"><br />رجل في الظلام تجاوز السبعين و بساقٍ مكسورة و يحتل الطابق الأرضي و في الأعلى ابنته الأرملة و حفيدته التي فقدت للأبد صديقها مؤخراً . و لأنه يشكو الأرق يعمد إلى سرد الكثير من القصص و اختلاقها و بهذه الطريقة يساعد عقارب الساعة على العمل أو على وجه الدقة يلهو عن مراقبة الوقت .<br /><br />يفضل العتمة على الضوء المزعج لعينيه ، في كل الأحوال ذاكرة أوغست بريل مضاءة بالكامل . يستيقظ بطل بريل في حفرة لا يمكنه الخروج منها و بعد لأي و يأس يخرجه أحدهم فيجد أمريكا تخوض حرباً و لكنها هذه المرة تخوضها ضد أمريكا نفسها . أوغست بريل نفسه تحدى الحرب العالمية و لكن سنّه الصغير حال دونها و لما أراد أن يتطوع حال دونه تشخيص طبي خاطيء و في زمن فيتنام كان قد تجاوز اوغست السن اللائقة للقتال . بطل قصة اوغست بريل على العكس من رجل الظلام لم يرغب في خوض حرب من أي نوع فهو ساحر ناجح و غير طموح يكفيه أن تبقى زوجته إلى جانبه . و كلما استشرى الأرق بأوغست دفع أوين بريك الساحر إلى لوثة الحروب . أوستر سينفرد بأصغر تفاصيل الحرب و سيتناول مقتل سائق في الحرب فقط . الحرب هنا حرب العراق و هناك تم نحر و بتسجيل فيديو عرض على مواقع النت صديق حفيدة أوغست و ستجد عزيزي القاريء هذا التقاطع الهندسي على طريقة غاودي ، ملفت للنظر هذه الطريقة التي كتب بها الرواية . عبقرية محضة تحسب لأوستر . لا يدور كل شيء حول الحرب ، سوف يقضي أوغست أوقاتاً مطولة مع حفيدته في مشاهدة الأفلام بشكل يومي محاولاً التخفيف عنها و ما أجمل تلك الأحاديث التي تبادلاها . سيحكي الكهل أيضاً قصة زواجه على مسمعها . الرواية جميلة جداً .</span>
  <a data-text-id="10233603689886977989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
